        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b04478"><meta property="og:title" content="Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–NaxCoO2 Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/acsami.7b04478.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Here, we take advantage of in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) to investigate the thermal stability of P2-type NaxCoO2 cathode materials for sodium ion batteries, which are promising candidates for next-generation lithium ion batteries. A double-tilt TEM heating holder was used to directly characterize the changes in the morphology and the crystallographic and electronic structures of the materials with increase in temperature. The electron diffraction patterns and the electron energy loss spectra demonstrated the presence of cobalt oxides (Co3O4, CoO) and even metallic cobalt (Co) at higher temperatures as a result of reduction of Co ions and loss of oxygen. The bright-field TEM images revealed that the surface of NaxCoO2 becomes porous at high temperatures. Higher cutoff voltages result in degrading thermal stability of NaxCoO2. The observations herein provide a valuable insight that thermal stability is one of the important factors to be considered in addition to the electrochemical properties when developing new electrode materials for novel battery systems."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/acsami.7b04478.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '2d470544-c5cb-4399-91f4-5fd138542b7d'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–Na&lt;sub&gt;&lt;italic&gt;x&lt;/italic&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;CoO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b04478", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–Na&lt;sub&gt;&lt;italic&gt;x&lt;/italic&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;CoO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b04478", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–Na&lt;sub&gt;&lt;italic&gt;x&lt;/italic&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;CoO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b04478", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–Na&lt;sub&gt;&lt;italic&gt;x&lt;/italic&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;CoO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b04478", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–Na&lt;sub&gt;&lt;italic&gt;x&lt;/italic&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;CoO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b04478", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–Na&lt;sub&gt;&lt;italic&gt;x&lt;/italic&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;CoO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b04478", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–Na&lt;sub&gt;&lt;italic&gt;x&lt;/italic&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;CoO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b04478", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–Na&lt;sub&gt;&lt;italic&gt;x&lt;/italic&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;CoO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b04478", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2–Na&lt;sub&gt;&lt;italic&gt;x&lt;/italic&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;CoO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b04478", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">61</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/medium/am-2017-04478c_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b04478&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Here, we take advantage of <i>in situ</i> transmission electron microscopy (TEM) to investigate the thermal stability of P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode materials for sodium ion batteries, which are promising candidates for next-generation lithium ion batteries. A double-tilt TEM heating holder was used to directly characterize the changes in the morphology and the crystallographic and electronic structures of the materials with increase in temperature. The electron diffraction patterns and the electron energy loss spectra demonstrated the presence of cobalt oxides (Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, CoO) and even metallic cobalt (Co) at higher temperatures as a result of reduction of Co ions and loss of oxygen. The bright-field TEM images revealed that the surface of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> becomes porous at high temperatures. Higher cutoff voltages result in degrading thermal stability of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>. The observations herein provide a valuable insight that thermal stability is one of the important factors to be considered in addition to the electrochemical properties when developing new electrode materials for novel battery systems.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.

                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Thermal+Stability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_thermal%20stability">thermal stability</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nacoo2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_NaCoO2">NaCoO<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Transmission+Electron+Microscopy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_transmission%20electron%20microscopy">transmission electron microscopy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" heating</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Despite the huge success of lithium ion batteries (LIBs), alternate power sources are in great demand because of the limited resources of lithium. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Research interests in replacing Li by more abundant element of Na, Mg, or Ca have been significantly growing from the viewpoint of sustainability. Among them, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are the most promising candidates for replacing the LIB technology. Na is one of the abundant elements on earth. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> In addition, both Li and Na are alkali metals; thus, they have similar chemical properties. Since the fundamental mechanisms of LIB can also be applied for SIB, we can take advantage of the well-established LIB technology to develop and improve the SIB technology. Owing to these virtues, SIBs have attracted increasing attention for large-scale energy storage applications.</div><div class="NLM_p">Sodium transition metal oxides (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>) are the most widely investigated cathode materials for SIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3 to 6">(3-6)</a> because lithium transition metal oxides (LiTMO<sub>2</sub>), representatively LiCoO<sub>2</sub>, are well-understood systems and have been utilized successfully for practical applications. While Li ions take octahedral sites in LiTMO<sub>2</sub>, Na ions can occupy either octahedral or prismatic sites, resulting in various kinds of oxygen stacking. For example, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> has O3, P2, and P3 structures depending on the Na content, where O and P indicate Na ions occupying the octahedral and prismatic sites, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7, 8)</a> Among these structures, the P2 structure is favored because of its higher energy density and longer cycle life. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 and 9">(6, 9)</a> Oxygen stacking in the P2 structure is ABBA while that in the O3 structure, adopted by LiTMO<sub>2</sub>, is ABCABC. These inherent dissimilarities in the crystal structures might induce different types of structural changes in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> compared to the well-known LiTMO<sub>2</sub> systems under various conditions. Therefore, these changes should be carefully considered because the structural properties of electrode materials are strongly correlated with the intercalation capability and the stability of the batteries.</div><div class="NLM_p">Previous studies on LiTMO<sub>2</sub> have demonstrated that the structure of LiTMO<sub>2</sub> can be modified during charge/discharge or with increase in the temperature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10-13)</a> Structural changes of LiTMO<sub>2</sub> at high temperatures are accompanied by oxygen evolution <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> which makes the whole battery system extremely dangerous due to reaction with flammable electrolytes. The thermal stability of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> has been investigated using a variety of techniques such as accelerating rate calorimetry, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref14" aria-label="reference 5 and 14">(5, 14)</a> differential scanning calorimetry, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> and time-resolved X-ray diffraction; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> however, issues related to thermal safety have not been fully assessed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Especially, consideration of local structural changes are lacking in previously reported studies even though local surface areas are subjected to deeper deintercalation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> and more violent thermal decomposition. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11, 12)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In the present work, we investigated the local changes in crystallographic and electronic structures as well as morphologies of charged P2–Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> with increase in temperature using real-time electron microcopy. The material P2–Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> was selected as a model system of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> because of its chemical similarity with LiCoO<sub>2</sub>, the most popular cathode material for LIBs, and the superior electrochemical properties of P2-structured Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> cathode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 and 9">(6, 9)</a> The surface areas of cathode materials are of particular interest since the local surface area experiences harsher conditions than the bulk due to the overpotential developed at the interface between the cathode material and the electrolyte. Using electron microscopy, we can observe the development of porosity with modifications in the crystallographic and electronic structures while heating P2–Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> after initial charging. As the temperature increases, desodiated Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> decomposes into cobalt oxides and even into metallic Co. As most of the Na ions are extracted from the structure, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> becomes thermally unstable, resulting in thermal decomposition at lower temperatures. This study presents the possible safety threats of P2–Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, which should be overcome for practical applications.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Experimental Details</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">P2–Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> (NCO) raw material was synthesized by solid-state reaction. Stoichiometric amounts of sodium carbonate (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) and cobalt oxide (Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) were well ground using a mortar and pestle, and then, the mixtures were pelletized, followed by heat treatment at 850 °C for 24 h under air flow. The weight percent of Na and Co of the as-synthesized material was determined using an atomic absorption spectrometer (ICE 3000 series, Thermos). The bulk crystal structure of NCO was investigated by X-ray diffraction (XRD) which was performed with a D-max 2500/PC X-ray diffractometer (Rigaku) using Cu Kα radiation over a 2θ range of 10–70° at a scan rate of 2°/min.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Electrochemical Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The NCO cathode materials were electrochemically charged at a rate of 10 mA/g at constant current condition until the cell potential reached 3.5, 4.1, and 4.3 V. The cathode part was prepared from the slurry of 80 wt % of active material (NCO), 10 wt % of conducting carbon (Super P), and 10 wt % of polyvinylidene fluoride binder in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone solvent. This slurry was then coated onto an Al foil, which acted as a current collector. A 2032 type coin cell was assembled using the prepared cathode part, Na metal as the anode, a glass fiber filter (GF/F, Whatman) as the separator, and an electrolyte of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in a solvent of ethylene carbonate, diethyl carbonate, and propylene carbonate (1:1:1 by volume).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Sample Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">After attaining the required cutoff voltages (3.5, 4.1, and 4.3 V), the coin cells were disassembled, and the cathode was cleaned with pure dimethyl carbonate (DMC) solution to remove the residual salts. The charged NCO particles were acquired by abrading the Al foil. The powder samples were dispersed in pure DMC solvent before dropping onto a lacey carbon TEM grid. All the sample preparation processes were carried out in an argon-filled glovebox to minimize the exposure of the sample to air and moisture.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> In Situ TEM Experiments</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The changes in the structures of the NCO cathode materials during heating were observed in real time using Tecnai G<sup>2</sup> F20 (FEI) TEM at an accelerating voltage of 120 kV. A 652 double-tilt heating holder (Gatan) was utilized for increasing the temperature from room temperature (25 °C) to 400 °C. The electron energy loss (EEL) spectra were acquired using a GIF Tridiem system (Gatan). The energy resolution of the EEL spectrum, as determined by measuring the full-width at half-maximum of the zero-loss peak, was around 1 eV. The backgrounds of all the spectra were subtracted using the power law method embedded in Digital Micrograph software (Gatan).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium cobalt oxide (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>, NCO) active cathode material was synthesized by the solid-state method. The composition <i>x</i> was determined to be 0.69 by atomic absorption spectroscopy (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a) shows that the primary particles of the as-synthesized NCO material exhibit plate-like morphologies. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b shows the X-ray diffraction pattern of the pristine NCO material, which matches that of P2–Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> reference (JCPDS #30–1182), demonstrating that the as-synthesized NCO has P2 structure. To investigate the thermal stability of the NCO material depending on the remaining sodium content, we controlled the cutoff voltages (3.5, 4.1, and 4.3 V). The charge profiles of the NCO cathode material are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c. The initial charge curve to 4.3 V shows multiple plateaus at 2.7, 2.95, 3.3, 3.6, and 4.0 V, indicating that the sodium deintercalation process undergoes complicated series of phase transitions. When charged from the initial composition of NCO up to 3.5, 4.1, and 4.3 V, the materials delivered a charge capacity of 45.8, 116.1, and 146.8 mAh/g, respectively, corresponding to the formation of Na<sub>0.52</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.24</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>0.12</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>, assuming that all the electrons moving out of the NCO cathode are compensated by the increase in the valence of Co with sodium deintercalation. After the initial charging with different cutoff voltages, the bulk crystal structures of the NCO materials were examined by XRD (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d). Compared to the XRD pattern of the pristine material, peak splitting, emerging, or disappearing was not observed in the XRD patterns of the charged materials, indicating that the bulk structures are well maintained after the initial charge. Meanwhile, diffraction peak shift is observable and the changes in lattice parameters are summarized at Figure S1 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/medium/am-2017-04478c_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) SEM image and (b) X-ray diffraction pattern of as-synthesized NCO cathode material. (c) Charge profiles of NCO cathode materials with cutoff voltages of 3.5, 4.1, and 4.3 V. (d) X-ray diffraction patterns of NCO cathode materials before and after initial charge.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b04478&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Changes in the morphologies of the NCO materials during heating were tracked by recording a series of bright field images, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. As the temperature increased, the surface areas of the NCO materials became porous, implying that gas evolution had occurred. The morphologies of the NCO materials changed significantly with increase in the cutoff voltages or decrease in the Na ions remaining in the original structure. Figure S2 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> shows how morphological changes occurred at the whole NCO particles upon heating, which indicates that thermal degradation initiates at the edge of the particles and propagates toward the bulk and the degree of thermal decomposition becomes more severe with the depth of charge. The crystal phases at each temperature were determined from the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>). Details regarding the indexing of the SAED patterns are provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. At high temperatures, the surface of desodiated Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> decomposed into cobalt oxides (Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, CoO) or even metallic Co. The NCO cathode materials charged to higher voltages became more thermally unstable, resulting in the degradation of NCO at lower temperatures. For example, the Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> phase was found even at 100 °C in the case of the sample charged to 4.3 V, whereas for the sample charged to 3.5 V, the thermal decomposition to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was apparent only at 400 °C. Sodium ordering between CoO<sub>2</sub> layers has been reported to take place at a certain sodium content (<i>x</i> = 0.5 in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>) and temperature range (410–470 K), resulting in additional diffraction spots of a superstructure hexagonal phase with doubled cell parameters in the <i>ab</i> plane. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> We observed this superstructure in the samples charged at 4.1 and 4.3 V at 200 °C (473 K). Faint diffraction spots from the superstructure were also observed in the 3.5 V-charged NCO at 300 °C; we speculate that this sodium ordering is not fully relaxed at temperatures higher than the previously reported temperature range.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/medium/am-2017-04478c_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Real-time BF images of the surface of NCO cathode materials charged to (a) 3.5 V, (b) 4.1 V, and (c) 4.3 V during heating.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b04478&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/medium/am-2017-04478c_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Real-time electron diffraction patterns acquired from the surface of NCO cathode materials charged to (a) 3.5 V, (b) 4.1 V, and (c) 4.3 V during heating. NCO* indicates the NCO phase with the sodium ordering.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b04478&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The variations in the electronic structures of the NCO cathode materials during heating were investigated by electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS), which reflects the density of states above the Fermi level. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Electron microscopy allows us to investigate the electronic and the crystallographic structures simultaneously, which is essential to elucidate the direct relationship between thermal decomposition and chemistry of materials during heating. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> shows the O K-edge and the Co L<sub>2,3</sub>-edge EEL spectra of the NCO cathode materials at different states of charging with increase in the temperature. The intensity of each spectrum is normalized with the highest intensity of O K- or L<sub>3</sub>-edge of Co L<sub>2,3</sub>-edge. In the case of the 4.3 V-charged sample, the O K-edge at 400 °C was exaggerated as a result of the intensity normalization. The raw EEL spectrum is provided in Figure S4 in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, which shows that the O K-edge almost vanished at 400 °C. The O K-edge is mainly composed of two prominent peaks: a pre-edge at ∼530 eV, which originates from the electron transition from 1s to the hybridized state of O 2p with localized 3d of transition metal (TM, in this case Co), and another above 540 eV, which is attributed to the electron transition to the hybridized state of O 2p with 4sp of TM. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> The Co L-edge is derived from the transition from 2p to the highly localized 3d state. Spin–orbit coupling splits the degeneracy of the 2p state into 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> levels, which results in two independent L<sub>2</sub> and L<sub>3</sub> edges. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> As the temperature increases, both the O K- and Co L-edges change: a positive shift in the pre-edge of the O K-edge, a decrease in the onset energy of the Co L<sub>2,3</sub>-edge, and changes in the L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> intensity ratio of Co. All these changes arise from the reduction of Co. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref21" aria-label="reference 10 and 21">(10, 21)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/medium/am-2017-04478c_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. O K-edge and Co L<sub>2,3</sub> edge EEL spectra of NCO cathode materials charged to (a) 3.5 V, (b) 4.1 V, and (c) 4.3 V during heating.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b04478&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The modifications in the electronic structures of the NCO materials upon heating are presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. We utilized two indexes of Δ<i>E</i> and L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> intensity ratio to express the changes in O K- and Co L-edges, respectively. The index Δ<i>E</i> corresponds to the energy loss difference at the highest intensities of the pre-edge and the main peaks of the O K-edge. As the Co ions binding with oxygen reduce, the pre-edge moves to higher values of energy loss, resulting in a decrease in the Δ<i>E.</i> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> The threshold energy of the L<sub>2,3</sub>-edge and the L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> intensity ratio reflect the oxidation state of the TM. However, we used the L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> intensity ratio only for tracking the chemical state of Co because the energy resolution of the electron beam (∼1 eV) may lead to misinterpretation of the chemical shift. As the temperature increases, the Δ<i>E</i> decreases while the L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> intensity ratio generally increases. In other words, reduction of Co occurs with increase in the temperature, and the chemical state changes severely at lower temperatures for higher cutoff voltages. The 4.3 V-charged NCO additionally exhibited an unusual trend in the L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> ratio; it continuously increased until 300 °C and then decreased at 400 °C. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> shows that the O K-edge of the 4.3 V-charged NCO at 400 °C almost disappears, implying that most of the oxygen is released from the NCO materials in order to fulfill the charge neutrality due to the reduction of Co. In the absence of oxygen, the Co ions are ultimately reduced to the metallic state. The L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> ratio of metallic Co is lower than that of the ionized state. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> The L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> ratios of different oxidation states of Co are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> and have been plotted based on ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">22</a>. Therefore, the unusual decrease in the L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> ratio of the 4.3 V-charged NCO at 400 °C originates from the ultimate reduction of Co ion to metallic Co. All the changes in the electronic structures are well matched to those in the crystallographic structures. The thermal decomposition from Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, CoO, and eventually Co occurs with the continuous reduction of Co ions and the loss of oxygen, and the appearance of metallic Co was confirmed by both SAED and EELS results.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/medium/am-2017-04478c_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Changes in (a) Δ<i>E</i> of O K-edge and (b) L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> intensity ratio of Co L edge with increasing temperature.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b04478&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> summarizes schematically the changes occurred in charged NCO cathode materials with the temperature range from the room temperature to 400 °C. At higher temperatures, NCO cathode materials thermally decomposed to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, CoO, and Co with Co reduction. With higher cutoff voltages, more severe Co reduction happens at lower temperature. In addition, nascent porosity initiates at the edge of the particle and propagates into the bulk area. Compared to our previous research on cathode materials for LIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12, 13)</a> the charged NCO materials in the present study exhibit similar problems of structural modifications and resultant oxygen loss at high temperatures. As sodium ion batteries are being targeted for large-scale applications, the electrode materials must be thermally stable because both the possibility of failure in heat management and the impact of unwanted heat can be significantly increased in large units of batteries. Therefore, the P2 type cathode materials should be modified to improve the thermal stability. Methods such as surface coating, adding and optimizing transition metal compositions into the structure, or controlling the crystal structure are believed to be effective in suppressing the thermal decomposition of P2–Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials for sodium ion batteries.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/medium/am-2017-04478c_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Schematic representation summarizing thermal decomposition process of charged NCO with different depth of charging depending on the temperatures.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/asset/images/large/am-2017-04478c_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b04478&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we used real-time transmission electron microscopy to examine the thermal stability of P2–Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode materials for sodium ion batteries. With an increase in temperature, the surface of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> became porous due to the thermal decomposition to Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, CoO, and Co with the reduction of Co. The degree of the thermal decomposition increased at higher cutoff voltages at the same temperature. At the harshest condition of 4.3 V cutoff voltage and 400 °C used in this study, significant changes were observed in the morphology as well as the crystallographic and the electronic structures as a result of the reduction of Co to the metallic state and the loss of oxygen, which are serious safety threats to the life of the battery system. This study demonstrates the instability of charged P2-type layered cathode materials at high temperatures. The thermal stability of electrode materials should be considerably improved in order to guarantee the safe operation of sodium ion batteries, and this work provides an excellent methodology to verify the thermal stability of newly developed cathode materials.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b04478" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b04478</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Further information on atomic absorption spectroscopy results; XRD, SAED, and EELS analysis; and bright-field images of whole NCO particles upon heating (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf">am7b04478_si_001.pdf (741.72 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b04478" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Energy Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">2</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">710</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">721</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem. Rev.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">114</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">11636</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">11682</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/cr500192f</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D114%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D11636-11682%26journal%3DChem.%2BRev.%26author%3DN.%2BYabuuchi%26author%3DK.%2BKubota%26author%3DM.%2BDahbi%26author%3DS.%2BKomaba%26title%3DResearch%2BDevelopment%2Bon%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fcr500192f&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b04478&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fcr500192f&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Kubota, Kei; Dahbi, Mouad; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">114</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of sodium-ion battery technol.  Future outlook of sodium-ion batteries is discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/nmat2920"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Berthelot, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Carlier, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Delmas, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Investigation of the P2-Na<sub>x</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> Phase Diagram</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical investigation of the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Berthelot, R.; Carlier, D.; Delmas, C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na layered oxides NaxCoO2 form one of the most fascinating low-dimensional and strongly correlated systems; in particular P2-NaxCoO2 exhibits various single-phase domains with different Na+/vacancy patterns depending on the Na concn.  Here the authors used Na batteries to clearly depict the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram for x ≥ 0.50.  By coupling the electrochem. process with an in situ x-ray diffraction expt., the authors identified the succession of single-phase or two-phase domains appearing on Na intercalation with a rather good accuracy compared with previous studies.  The authors reported new single-phase domains and the authors underlined the thermal instability of some ordered phases from an electrochem. study at various temps.  As each phase was characterized by the position of its Fermi level vs. the Na+/Na couple, the synthesis of each material, even in large amts., can be carried out electrochem.  The phys. properties of the as-prepd. Na1/2CoO2 and Na2/3CoO2 ordered phases were characterized and compared.  Electrochem. processes are an accurate route to precisely study in a continuous way such a complex system and provide a new way to synthesize materials with a very narrow existence range.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3309"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kajiyama, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Iwatate, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nishikawa, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hitomi, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Okuyama, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Usui, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamada, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-Type Na<sub>x</sub>[Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Made From Earth-Abundant Elements for Rechargeable Na Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li batteries have risen to prominence as key devices for green and sustainable energy development.  Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported. Herein, the authors report a new electrode material, P2-Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2, that delivers 190 mA-h/g of reversible capacity in Na cells with the electrochem. active Fe3+/Fe4+ redox.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Voltage, stability and diffusion barrier differences between sodium-ion and lithium-ion intercalation materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ong, Shyue Ping; Chevrier, Vincent L.; Hautier, Geoffroy; Jain, Anubhav; Moore, Charles; Kim, Sangtae; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3680-3688</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To evaluate the potential of Na-ion batteries, we contrast in this work the difference between Na-ion and Li-ion based intercalation chemistries in terms of three key battery properties-voltage, phase stability and diffusion barriers.  The compds. investigated comprise the layered AMO2 and AMS2 structures, the olivine and maricite AMPO4 structures, and the NASICON A3V2(PO4)3 structures.  The calcd. Na voltages for the compds. investigated are 0.18-0.57 V lower than that of the corresponding Li voltages, in agreement with previous exptl. data.  We believe the obsd. lower voltages for Na compds. are predominantly a cathodic effect related to the much smaller energy gain from inserting Na into the host structure compared to inserting Li.  We also found a relatively strong dependence of battery properties on structural features.  In general, the difference between the Na and Li voltage of the same structure, ΔVNa-Li, is less neg. for the maricite structures preferred by Na, and more neg. for the olivine structures preferred by Li.  The layered compds. have the most neg. ΔVNa-Li.  In terms of phase stability, we found that open structures, such as the layered and NASICON structures, that are better able to accommodate the larger Na+ ion generally have both Na and Li versions of the same compd.  For the close-packed AMPO4 structures, our results show that Na generally prefers the maricite structure, while Li prefers the olivine structure, in agreement with previous exptl. work.  We also found surprising evidence that the barriers for Na+ migration can potentially be lower than that for Li+ migration in the layered structures.  Overall, our findings indicate that Na-ion systems can be competitive with Li-ion systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1149/1.2095407"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shacklette, L.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rechargeable electrodes from sodium cobalt bronzes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shacklette, L.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Investigation of the Charge Compensation Mechanism on the Electrochemically Li-Ion Deintercalated Li1-xCo1/3Ni1/3Mn1/3O2 Electrode System by Combination of Soft and Hard X-ray Absorption Spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yoon, Won-Sub; Balasubramanian, Mahalingam; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Yang, Xiao-Qing; McBreen, James; Grey, Clare P.; Fischer, Daniel A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In situ hard x-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) at metal K-edges and soft XAS at O K-edge and metal L-edges were carried out during the 1st charging process for the layered Li1-xCo1/3Ni1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode material.  The metal K-edge XANES results show that the major charge compensation at the metal site during Li-ion deintercalation is achieved by the oxidn. of Ni2+ while the Mn and Co ions remain mostly unchanged as Mn4+ and Co3+.  These conclusions agree with the results of the metal K-edge EXAFS data.  Metal L-edge XAS results at different charge states in both the FY and PEY modes show that, unlike Mn and Co ions, Ni ions at the surface are oxidized to Ni3+ during charging, but Ni ions in the bulk are further oxidized to Ni4+.  From the observation of O K-edge XAS results, a large portion of the charge compensation during Li-ion deintercalation is achieved in the O site.  By comparison with earlier results on the Li1-xNi0.5Mn0.5O2 system, the active participation of O in the redox process in Li1-xCo1/3Ni1/3Mn1/3O2 is related to the presence of Co in this system.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1021/cm303096e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bak, S.-M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nam, K.-W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hwang, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stach, E.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, results are presented from the application of a new in situ technique that combines time-resolved synchrotron X-ray diffraction and mass spectroscopy.  This approach is exploited to provide direct correlation between structural changes and the evolution of gas that occurs during the thermal decompn. of (over)charged cathode materials used in lithium-ion batteries.  Results from charged LixNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2 cathode materials indicate that the evolution of both O2 and CO2 gases are strongly related to phase transitions that occur during thermal decompn., specifically from the layered structure (space group Fd‾3m) to the disordered spinel structure (Fd‾3m), and finally to the rock-salt structure (Fm‾3m).  The state of charge also significantly affects both the structural changes and the evolution of oxygen as the temp. increases: the more extensive the charge, the lower the temp. of the phase transitions and the larger the oxygen release.  Ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) are also utilized to investigate the local structural and valence state changes in Ni and Co ions, and to characterize microscopic morphol. changes.  The combination of these advanced tools provides a unique approach to study fundamental aspects of the dynamic phys. and chem. changes that occur during thermal decompn. of charged cathode materials in a systematic way.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/am503278f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hwang, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, S.
