                        cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy To Explore the Effects of Transition-Metal Composition on the Local Thermal Stability in Charged LixNiyMnzCo1-y-zO2 Cathode Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Sooyeon; Kim, Seung Min; Bak, Seong-Min; Kim, Se Young; Cho, Byung-Won; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Lee, Jeong Yong; Stach, Eric A.; Chang, Wonyoung</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3927-3935</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, we use in situ TEM to investigate the thermal decompn. that occurs at the surface of charged LixNiyMnzCo1-y-zO2 (NMC) cathode materials of different compn. (with y, z = 0.8, 0.1, and 0.6, 0.2, and 0.4,and 0.3), after they were charged to their practical upper limit voltage (4.3 V).  By heating these materials inside the TEM, we are able to directly characterize near surface changes in both their electronic structure (using electron energy loss spectroscopy) and crystal structure and morphol. (using electron diffraction and bright-field imaging).  The most Ni-rich material (y, z = 0.8, 0.1) is thermally unstable at significantly lower temps. than the other compns.-this is manifested by changes in both the electronic structure and the onset of phase transitions at temps. as low as 100°.  Electron energy loss spectroscopy indicates that (i) the thermally induced redn. of Ni ions drives these changes, and (ii) this is exacerbated by the presence of an addnl. redox reaction that occurs at 4.2 V in the y, z = 0.8, 0.1 material.  Exploration of individual particles shows that there are substantial variations in the onset temps. and overall extent of these changes.  Of the compns. studied, the compn. of y, z = 0.6, 0.2 has the optimal combination of high energy d. and reasonable thermal stability.  The observations herein demonstrate that real-time electron microscopy provide direct insight into the changes that occur in cathode materials with temp., allowing optimization of different alloy concns. to maximize overall performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1149/2.102205jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xia, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries offer a potential alternative or complementary system to lithium-ion batteries, which are widely used in many applications.  For this purpose, layered O3-type NaCrO2 for use as a cathode material in sodium-ion batteries was synthesized via an emulsion-drying method.  The produced NaCrO2 was modified using pitch as a carbon source and the products were tested in half and full cells using a NaPF6-based non-aq. electrolyte soln.  The carbon-coated NaCrO2 cathode material exhibits excellent capacity retention with superior rate capability up to a rate of 150 C (99 mA h (g oxide)-1), which corresponds to full discharge during 27 s.  The surface conducting carbon layer plays a critically important role in the excellent performance of this cathode material.  We confirmed the reaction process with sodium using X-ray diffraction and X-ray absorption spectroscopy.  Thermal anal. using time-resolved X-ray diffraction also demonstrated the structural stability of carbon-coated Na0.5CrO2.  Due to the excellent performance of the cathode material described herein, this study has the potential to promote the accelerated development of sodium-ion batteries for a large no. of applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1038/nmat4777"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Grey, C.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sustainability and in situ monitoring in battery development</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grey C P; Grey C P; Tarascon J M; Tarascon J M; Tarascon J M</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature materials</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of improved rechargeable batteries represents a major technological challenge for this new century, as batteries constitute the limiting components in the shift from petrol (gasoline) powered to electric vehicles, while also enabling the use of more renewable energy on the grid.  To minimize the ecological implications associated with their wider use, we must integrate sustainability of battery materials into our research endeavours, choosing chemistries that have a minimum footprint in nature and that are more readily recycled or integrated into a full circular economy.  Sustainability and cost concerns require that we greatly increase the battery lifetime and consider second lives for batteries.  As part of this, we must monitor the state of health of batteries continuously during operation to minimize their degradation.  It is thus important to push the frontiers of operando techniques to monitor increasingly complex processes.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Investigation of Changes in the Surface Structure of LixNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2 Cathode Materials Induced by the Initial Charge</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Sooyeon; Chang, Wonyoung; Kim, Seung Min; Su, Dong; Kim, Dong Hyun; Lee, Jeong Yong; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Stach, Eric A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">We use transmission electron microscopy (TEM) to investigate the evolution of the surface structure of LixNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2 cathode materials as a function of the extent of first charge at room temp. using a combination of high-resoln. electron microscopy imaging, selected area electron diffraction, and electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS).  It was found that the surface changes from the layered structure (space group R‾3m) to the disordered spinel structure (Fd‾3m), and eventually to the rock-salt structure (Fm‾3m), and that these changes are more substantial as the extent of charge increases.  EELS indicates that these crystal structure changes are also accompanied by significant changes in the electronic structure, which are consistent with delithiation leading to both a redn. of the Ni and an increase in the effective electron d. of oxygen.  This leads to a charge imbalance, which results in the formation of oxygen vacancies and the development of surface porosity.  The degree of local surface structure change differs among particles, likely due to kinetic factors that are manifested with changes in particle size.  These results demonstrate that TEM, when coupled with EELS, can provide detailed information about the crystallog. and electronic structure changes that occur at the surface of these materials during delithiation.  This information is of crit. importance for obtaining a complete understanding of the mechanisms by which both degrdn. and thermal runaway initiate in these electrode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1016/j.ssc.2005.02.006"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H. X.; Nie, C. J.; Shi, Y. G.; Yu, H. C.; Ding, S.; Liu, Y. L.; Wu, D.; Wang, N. L.; Li, J. Q.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The nonstoichiometric NaxCoO2 system exhibits extraordinary phys. properties that correlate with temp. and Na concn. in its layered lattice without evident long-range structure modification when conventional crystallog. techniques are applied.  For instance, Na0.7CoO2, a thermodynamically stable phase, shows large thermoelec. power; water-intercalated Na0.33CoO2·1.3H2O is a newly discovered superconductor with T c∼4 K, and Na0.5CoO2 exhibits an unexpected charge ordering transition at around T co∼55 K Recent studies suggest that the transport and magnetic properties in the NaxCoO2 system strongly depend on the charge carrier d. and local structural properties.  Here the authors report a combined variable temp. TEM and Raman scattering study on structural transformations in Na0.5CoO2 single crystals.  Structural phase transitions at 80-1000 K are directly identified and the obsd. superstructures and modulated phases can be interpreted by Na-ordering.  The Raman scattering measurements reveal phase sepn. and a systematic evolution of active modes along with phase transitions.  The authors' work demonstrates that the high mobility and ordering of sodium cations among the CoO2 layers are a key factor for the presence of complex structural properties in NaxCoO2 materials, and also demonstrate that the combination of electron diffraction and Raman spectroscopy measurements is an efficient way for studying the cation ordering and phase transitions in related systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1046/j.1365-2818.2001.00898.x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Keast, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Scott, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review with many refs.  Electron energy-loss near-edge structure (ELNES) is a technique that can be used to measure the electronic structure (i.e. bonding) in materials with subnanometer spatial resoln.  This review covers the theor. principles behind the technique, the exptl. procedures necessary to acquire good ELNES spectra, including potential artifacts, and gives examples relevant to materials science.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.69.235103"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Graetz, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ahn, C. C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ouyang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Rez, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fultz, B.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">White Lines and D-Band Occupancy for the 3d Transition-Metal Oxides and Lithium Transition-Metal Oxides</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Phys. Rev. B: Condens. Matter Mater. Phys.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2004</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">69</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">235103</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1103/PhysRevB.69.235103</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D69%26publication_year%3D2004%26pages%3D235103%26journal%3DPhys.%2BRev.%2BB%253A%2BCondens.%2BMatter%2BMater.%2BPhys.%26author%3DJ.%2BGraetz%26author%3DC.%2BC.%2BAhn%26author%3DH.%2BOuyang%26author%3DP.%2BRez%26author%3DB.%2BFultz%26title%3DWhite%2BLines%2Band%2BD-Band%2BOccupancy%2Bfor%2Bthe%2B3d%2BTransition-Metal%2BOxides%2Band%2BLithium%2BTransition-Metal%2BOxides%26doi%3D10.1103%252FPhysRevB.69.235103&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b04478&amp;doiOfLink=10.1103%2FPhysRevB.69.235103&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">White lines and d-band occupancy for the 3d transition-metal oxides and lithium transition-metal oxides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Graetz, Jason; Ahn, Channing C.; Ouyang, Hao; Rez, Peter; Fultz, Brent</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B: Condensed Matter and Materials Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2004</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">69</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">23</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">235103/1-235103/6</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRBMDO</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0163-1829</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Physical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electron energy-loss spectrometry was employed to measure the white lines at the L2,3 absorption edges of the 3d transition-metal oxides and lithium transition-metal oxides.  The white-line ratio (L3/L2) was found to increase between d0 and d5, and decrease between d5 and d10, consistent with previous results for the transition metals and their oxides.  The intensities of the white lines, normalized to the post-edge background, are linear for the 3d transition-metal and lithium transition-metal oxides.  An empirical correlation between normalized white-line intensity and 3d occupancy is established.  It provides a method for measuring changes in the 3d-state occupancy.  As an example, this empirical relationship is used to measure changes in the transition-metal valences of Li1-xNi0.8Co0.2O2 in the range of 0≤x≤0.64.  In these expts. the 3d occupancy of the nickel ion decreased upon lithium deintercalation, while the cobalt valence remained const.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1063/1.3482013"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Feltes, T. E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Regalbuto, J. R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Meyer, R. J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Klie, R. F.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In Situ Electron Energy Loss Spectroscopy Study of Metallic Co and Co Oxides</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Appl. Phys.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2010</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">108</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">063704</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1063/1.3482013</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D108%26publication_year%3D2010%26pages%3D063704%26journal%3DJ.%2BAppl.%2BPhys.%26author%3DY.%2BZhao%26author%3DT.%2BE.%2BFeltes%26author%3DJ.%2BR.%2BRegalbuto%26author%3DR.%2BJ.%2BMeyer%26author%3DR.%2BF.%2BKlie%26title%3DIn%2BSitu%2BElectron%2BEnergy%2BLoss%2BSpectroscopy%2BStudy%2Bof%2BMetallic%2BCo%2Band%2BCo%2BOxides%26doi%3D10.1063%252F1.3482013&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b04478&amp;doiOfLink=10.1063%2F1.3482013&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ electron energy loss spectroscopy study of metallic Co and Co oxides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Yuan; Feltes, Theresa E.; Regalbuto, John R.; Meyer, Randall J.; Klie, Robert F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Applied Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">108</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">063704/1-063704/7</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JAPIAU</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0021-8979</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Institute of Physics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Detg. the Co valence, particularly in Co-based nanocatalysts is a longstanding exptl. challenge.  In situ EELS and 1st-principles d. functional theory calcns. were used to distinguish between metallic Co, Co3O4, as well as CoO.  Differences in the O K- and Co L-edges are used to det. the Co valence in different Co-oxide particles.  While the metallic Co L3/L2-ratio is that of partially reduced Co3O4, the near-edge fine-structure of the metallic Co L-edge exhibits addnl. features not present in any Co-oxide.  The origin of these features are discussed.  Based on the exptl. and theor. results, a fitting method is proposed to distinguish metallic Co from Co-oxides.                         rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                 10.1038/nmat2920</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical investigation of the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Berthelot, R.; Carlier, D.; Delmas, C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na layered oxides NaxCoO2 form one of the most fascinating low-dimensional and strongly correlated systems; in particular P2-NaxCoO2 exhibits various single-phase domains with different Na+/vacancy patterns depending on the Na concn.  Here the authors used Na batteries to clearly depict the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram for x ≥ 0.50.  By coupling the electrochem. process with an in situ x-ray diffraction expt., the authors identified the succession of single-phase or two-phase domains appearing on Na intercalation with a rather good accuracy compared with previous studies.  The authors reported new single-phase domains and the authors underlined the thermal instability of some ordered phases from an electrochem. study at various temps.  As each phase was characterized by the position of its Fermi level vs. the Na+/Na couple, the synthesis of each material, even in large amts., can be carried out electrochem.  The phys. properties of the as-prepd. Na1/2CoO2 and Na2/3CoO2 ordered phases were characterized and compared.  Electrochem. processes are an accurate route to precisely study in a continuous way such a complex system and provide a new way to synthesize materials with a very narrow existence range.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3309"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kajiyama, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Iwatate, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nishikawa, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hitomi, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Okuyama, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Usui, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamada, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-Type Na<sub>x</sub>[Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Made From Earth-Abundant Elements for Rechargeable Na Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li batteries have risen to prominence as key devices for green and sustainable energy development.  Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported.  However, their reversible capacity is typically limited to 100 mA-h/g.  Herein, the authors report a new electrode material, P2-Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2, that delivers 190 mA-h/g of reversible capacity in Na cells with the electrochem. active Fe3+/Fe4+ redox.  These results will contribute to the development of rechargeable batteries from the earth-abundant elements operable at room temp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1149/2.002201esl"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xia, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical and Solid-State Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered structure NaCrO2 (R-3m) was successfully synthesized by solid state reaction.  The electrochem. performance of NaCrO2 as a pos. electrode material for sodium-ion batteries was tested at room temp. using two-electrode coin cells with NaClO4/PC electrolyte.  NaCrO2 delivered a reversible capacity of 110 mAh/g during the first charge and had good cyclability.  The reaction between deintercalated NaxCrO2 (∼Na0.5CrO2) and ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate solvent was studied using Accelerating Rate Calorimetry (ARC).  The results were compared to analogous results for Li0.5CoO2 and Li0FePO4 under the same conditions.  Na0.5CrO2 showed an unexpected excellent thermal stability in solvent with no heat release in the ARC test temp. range.  An X-ray diffraction study showed that Na0.5CrO2 decomps. to NaCrO2 and P3-type layered structure CrO2-δ with minimal oxygen release during the ARC tests, which explains the lack of exothermic behavior.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2012.09.058"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, J.                         rightTab-cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Voltage, stability and diffusion barrier differences between sodium-ion and lithium-ion intercalation materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ong, Shyue Ping; Chevrier, Vincent L.; Hautier, Geoffroy; Jain, Anubhav; Moore, Charles; Kim, Sangtae; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">To evaluate the potential of Na-ion batteries, we contrast in this work the difference between Na-ion and Li-ion based intercalation chemistries in terms of three key battery properties-voltage, phase stability and diffusion barriers.  The compds. investigated comprise the layered AMO2 and AMS2 structures, the olivine and maricite AMPO4 structures, and the NASICON A3V2(PO4)3 structures.  The calcd. Na voltages for the compds. investigated are 0.18-0.57 V lower than that of the corresponding Li voltages, in agreement with previous exptl. data.  We believe the obsd. lower voltages for Na compds. are predominantly a cathodic effect related to the much smaller energy gain from inserting Na into the host structure compared to inserting Li.  We also found a relatively strong dependence of battery properties on structural features.  In general, the difference between the Na and Li voltage of the same structure, ΔVNa-Li, is less neg. for the maricite structures preferred by Na, and more neg. for the olivine structures preferred by Li.  The layered compds. have the most neg. ΔVNa-Li.  In terms of phase stability, we found that open structures, such as the layered and NASICON structures, that are better able to accommodate the larger Na+ ion generally have both Na and Li versions of the same compd.  For the close-packed AMPO4 structures, our results show that Na generally prefers the maricite structure, while Li prefers the olivine structure, in agreement with previous exptl. work.  We also found surprising evidence that the barriers for Na+ migration can potentially be lower than that for Li+ migration in the layered structures.  Overall, our findings indicate that Na-ion systems can be competitive with Li-ion systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1149/1.2095407"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shacklette, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, results are presented from the application of a new in situ technique that combines time-resolved synchrotron X-ray diffraction and mass spectroscopy.  This approach is exploited to provide direct correlation between structural changes and the evolution of gas that occurs during the thermal decompn. of (over)charged cathode materials used in lithium-ion batteries.  Results from charged LixNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2 cathode materials indicate that the evolution of both O2 and CO2 gases are strongly related to phase transitions that occur during thermal decompn., specifically from the layered structure (space group Fd‾3m) to the disordered spinel structure (Fd‾3m), and finally to the rock-salt structure (Fm‾3m).  The state of charge also significantly affects both the structural changes and the evolution of oxygen as the temp. increases: the more extensive the charge, the lower the temp. of the phase transitions and the larger the oxygen release.  Ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) are also utilized to investigate the local structural and valence state changes in Ni and Co ions, and to characterize microscopic morphol. changes.  The combination of these advanced tools provides a unique approach to study fundamental aspects of the dynamic phys. and chem. changes that occur during thermal decompn. of charged cathode materials in a systematic way.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1021/am503278f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hwang, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, we use in situ TEM to investigate the thermal decompn. that occurs at the surface of charged LixNiyMnzCo1-y-zO2 (NMC) cathode materials of different compn. (with y, z = 0.8, 0.1, and 0.6, 0.2, and 0.4,and 0.3), after they were charged to their practical upper limit voltage (4.3 V).  By heating these materials inside the TEM, we are able to directly characterize near surface changes in both their electronic structure (using electron energy loss spectroscopy) and crystal structure and morphol. (using electron diffraction and bright-field imaging).  The most Ni-rich material (y, z = 0.8, 0.1) is thermally unstable at significantly lower temps. than the other compns.-this is manifested by changes in both the electronic structure and the onset of phase transitions at temps. as low as 100°.  Electron energy loss spectroscopy indicates that (i) the thermally induced redn. of Ni ions drives these changes, and (ii) this is exacerbated by the presence of an addnl. redox reaction that occurs at 4.2 V in the y, z = 0.8, 0.1 material.  Exploration of individual particles shows that there are substantial variations in the onset temps. and overall extent of these changes.  Of the compns. studied, the compn. of y, z = 0.6, 0.2 has the optimal combination of high energy d. and reasonable thermal stability.  The observations herein demonstrate that real-time electron microscopy provide direct insight into the changes that occur in cathode materials with temp., allowing optimization of different alloy concns. to maximize overall performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref14" class="rightTabRefNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit14" data-doi="10.1149/2.102205jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xia, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J. For this purpose, layered O3-type NaCrO2 for use as a cathode material in sodium-ion batteries was synthesized via an emulsion-drying method.  The produced NaCrO2 was modified using pitch as a carbon source and the products were tested in half and full cells using a NaPF6-based non-aq. electrolyte soln.  The carbon-coated NaCrO2 cathode material exhibits excellent capacity retention with superior rate capability up to a rate of 150 C (99 mA h (g oxide)-1), which corresponds to full discharge during 27 s.  The surface conducting carbon layer plays a critically important role in the excellent performance of this cathode material.  We confirmed the reaction process with sodium using X-ray diffraction and X-ray absorption spectroscopy.  Thermal anal. using time-resolved X-ray diffraction also demonstrated the structural stability of carbon-coated Na0.5CrO2. To minimize the ecological implications associated with their wider use, we must integrate sustainability of battery materials into our research endeavours, choosing chemistries that have a minimum footprint in nature and that are more readily recycled or integrated into a full circular economy.  Sustainability and cost concerns require that we greatly increase the battery lifetime and consider second lives for batteries.  As part of this, we must monitor the state of health of batteries continuously during operation to minimize their degradation.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We use transmission electron microscopy (TEM) to investigate the evolution of the surface structure of LixNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2 cathode materials as a function of the extent of first charge at room temp. using a combination of high-resoln. electron microscopy imaging, selected area electron diffraction, and electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS).  It was found that the surface changes from the layered structure (space group R‾3m) to the disordered spinel structure (Fd‾3m), and eventually to the rock-salt structure (Fm‾3m), and that these changes are more substantial as the extent of charge increases. The degree of local surface structure change differs among particles, likely due to kinetic factors that are manifested with changes in particle size.  These results demonstrate that TEM, when coupled with EELS, can provide detailed information about the crystallog. and electronic structure changes that occur at the surface of these materials during delithiation. While the metallic Co L3/L2-ratio is that of partially reduced Co3O4, the near-edge fine-structure of the metallic Co L-edge exhibits addnl. features not present in any Co-oxide.  The origin of these features are discussed.  Based on the exptl. and theor. results, a fitting method is proposed to distinguish metallic Co from Co-oxides.  (c) 2010 American Institute of Physics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i9">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b04478" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b04478</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Further information on atomic absorption spectroscopy results; XRD, SAED, and EELS analysis; and bright-field images of whole NCO particles upon heating (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b04478/suppl_file/am7b04478_si_001.pdf">am7b04478_si_001.pdf (741.72 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

        <script nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC">
// LIT-455776 workaround
// Moving the popup before the header content for tab accessibility
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', () => {
    const headerNode = $('.header_content');
    const popupNode = $('.pairing-affiliations-popup');

    if (!headerNode.length || !popupNode.length)
        return;

    popupNode.insertBefore(headerNode);

<script nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });

        init: init,
      };
    })();

        <script nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="9318874d8e5d54b8-SJC">
$(document).ready(function(){
    var cookie = "";
    var uri = "/doi/full/10.1021/html_original_uri?id=123";
    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
        console.log("ACSPubs2 failed");
        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
});
